<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2642" data-file-width="4144" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG/200px-Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG/300px-Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG/400px-Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_welcome_sign_at_Madison_Parish_visitor_center_IMG_7059.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Louisiana entrance sign off <a href="/wiki/Interstate_20" title="Interstate 20">Interstate 20</a> in <a href="/wiki/Madison_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Madison Parish, Louisiana">Madison Parish</a> east of <a href="/wiki/Tallulah,_Louisiana" title="Tallulah, Louisiana">Tallulah</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Louisiana</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[a]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">state</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a>. Louisiana is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_area" title="List of U.S. states and territories by area">31st most extensive</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_population" title="List of U.S. states and territories by population">25th most populous</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states" title="List of U.S. states">50 United States</a>. Its capital is <a href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge,_Louisiana" title="Baton Rouge, Louisiana">Baton Rouge</a> and largest city is <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. Louisiana is the only state in the U.S. with political subdivisions termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parishes_of_Louisiana" title="Parishes of Louisiana">parishes</a>, which are the local government's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County-equivalent" title="County-equivalent">equivalent to counties</a>. The largest parish by population is <a href="/wiki/East_Baton_Rouge_Parish,_Louisiana" title="East Baton Rouge Parish, Louisiana">East Baton Rouge Parish</a>, and the largest by total area is <a href="/wiki/Plaquemines_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Plaquemines Parish, Louisiana">Plaquemines</a>. Louisiana is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> to the west, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> to the south.</p>
<p>Much of the state's lands were formed from sediment washed down the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>, leaving enormous deltas and vast areas of coastal marsh and swamp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddy_2008_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keddy_2008-11">[10]</a></sup> These contain a rich southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">biota</a>; typical examples include birds such as <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">ibis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egret" title="Egret">egrets</a>. There are also many species of <a href="/wiki/Tree_frog" title="Tree frog">tree frogs</a>, and fish such as <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paddlefish" title="Paddlefish">paddlefish</a>. In more elevated areas, fire is a natural process in the landscape, and has produced extensive areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longleaf_pine" title="Longleaf pine">longleaf pine</a> forest and wet <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savannas</a>. These support an exceptionally large number of plant species, including many species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orchids" title="Orchids">orchids</a> and carnivorous plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddy_2008_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keddy_2008-11">[10]</a></sup> Louisiana has more Native American tribes than any other southern state, including four that are federally recognized, ten that are state recognized, and four that have not yet received recognition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some Louisiana urban environments have a <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multicultural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">multilingual</a> heritage, being so strongly influenced by a mixture of 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_France" title="Culture of France">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Spain" title="Culture of Spain">Spanish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_recognized_tribes#Louisiana" title="State recognized tribes">Native American</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Africa" title="Culture of Africa">African</a> cultures that they are considered to be exceptional in the US. Before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" title="United States of America">American</a> <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">purchase</a> of the territory in 1803, the current Louisiana State had been both a <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">French</a> colony and for a brief period, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">Spanish</a> one. In addition, colonists imported numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_ethnic_groups_of_Africa" title="Native ethnic groups of Africa">African</a> people as slaves in the 18th century. Many came from peoples of the same region of West Africa, thus concentrating their culture. In the post-Civil War environment, Anglo-Americans increased the pressure for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicization" title="Anglicization">Anglicization</a>, and in 1921, English was for a time made the sole language of instruction in Louisiana schools before a policy of multilingualism was revived in 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouisianaOfficialSiteLanguage_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-LouisianaOfficialSiteLanguage-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-14">[13]</a></sup> There has never been an official language in Louisiana, and the state constitution enumerates "the right of the people to preserve, foster, and promote their respective historic, linguistic, and cultural origins," whether English, French, Spanish, or otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouisianaOfficialSiteLanguage_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-LouisianaOfficialSiteLanguage-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Louisiana was named after <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a>, King of France from 1643 to 1715. When <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9-Robert_Cavelier,_Sieur_de_La_Salle" title="René-Robert Cavelier, Sieur de La Salle">René-Robert Cavelier, Sieur de La Salle</a> claimed the territory drained by the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> for France, he named it <span lang="fr"><i>La Louisiane</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> The suffix -ana (or -ane) is a Latin suffix that can refer to "information relating to a particular individual, subject, or place." Thus, roughly, Louis + ana carries the idea of "related to Louis." Once part of the French Colonial Empire, the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a> stretched from present-day Mobile Bay to just north of the present-day <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada–United States border">Canada–United States border</a>, including a small part of what is now the Canadian provinces of <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River_Delta" title="Mississippi River Delta">Mississippi River Delta</a></div>
<p>The Gulf of Mexico did not exist 250 million years ago when there was but one supercontinent, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pangea" title="Pangea">Pangea</a>. As Pangea split apart, the Atlantic Ocean and Gulf of Mexico opened. Louisiana slowly developed, over millions of years, from water into land, and from north to south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddy_2008_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keddy_2008-11">[10]</a></sup> The oldest rocks are exposed in the north, in areas such as the Kisatchie National Forest. The oldest rocks date back to the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cenozoic_Era" title="Cenozoic Era">Cenozoic Era</a>, some 60 million years ago. The history of the formation of these rocks can be found in D. Spearing's <i>Roadside Geology of Louisiana</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The youngest parts of the state were formed during the last 12,000 years as successive deltas of the Mississippi River: the Maringouin, Teche, St. Bernard, Lafourche, the modern Mississippi, and now the Atchafalaya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The sediments were carried from north to south by the Mississippi River.</p>
<p>In between the Tertiary rocks of the north, and the relatively new sediments along the coast, is a vast belt known as the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> Terraces. Their age and distribution can be largely related to the rise and fall of sea levels during past ice ages. In general, the northern terraces have had sufficient time for rivers to cut deep channels, while the newer terraces tend to be much flatter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Salt_dome" title="Salt dome">Salt domes</a> are also found in Louisiana. Their origin can be traced back to the early Gulf of Mexico, when the shallow ocean had high rates of evaporation. There are several hundred salt domes in the state; one of the most familiar is Avery Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Salt domes are important not only as a source of salt; they also serve as underground traps for oil and gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Louisiana is bordered to the west by <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>; to the north by <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>; to the east by the state of <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>; and to the south by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>. The surface of the state may properly be divided into two parts, the uplands of the north, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alluvial_deposit" title="Alluvial deposit">alluvial</a> along the coast.</p>
<p>The alluvial region includes low swamp lands, coastal marshlands and beaches, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrier_islands" title="Barrier islands">barrier islands</a> that cover about 20,000 square miles (52,000 km<sup>2</sup>). This area lies principally along the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>, which traverses the state from north to south for a distance of about 600 miles (1,000 km) and empties into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_South" title="Red River of the South">Red River</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Ouachita_River" title="Ouachita River">Ouachita River</a> and its branches; and other minor streams (some of which are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayous" title="Bayous">bayous</a>).</p>
<p>The breadth of the alluvial region along the Mississippi is from 10 to 60 miles (15 to 100 km), and along the other rivers, the alluvial region averages about 10 miles (15 km) across. The Mississippi River flows along a ridge formed by its own natural deposits (known as a <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levee</a>), from which the lands decline toward a river beyond at an average fall of six feet per mile (3 m/km). The alluvial lands along other streams present similar features.</p>
<p>The higher and contiguous hill lands of the north and northwestern part of the state have an area of more than 25,000 square miles (65,000 km<sup>2</sup>). They consist of prairie and woodlands. The elevations above sea level range from 10 feet (3 m) at the coast and swamp lands to 50 and 60 feet (15–18 m) at the prairie and alluvial lands. In the uplands and hills, the elevations rise to <a href="/wiki/Driskill_Mountain" title="Driskill Mountain">Driskill Mountain</a>, the highest point in the state at only 535 feet (163 m) above sea level. From years 1932 to 2010 the state lost 1,800 sq. miles due to rises in sea level and <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Coastal_Protection_and_Restoration_Authority" title="Louisiana Coastal Protection and Restoration Authority">Louisiana Coastal Protection and Restoration Authority (CPRA)</a> spends around $1 billion per year to help shore up and protect Louisiana <a href="/wiki/Shore" title="Shore">shoreline</a> and land in both federal and state funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides the waterways already named, there are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabine_River_(Texas-Louisiana)" title="Sabine River (Texas-Louisiana)">Sabine</a>, forming the western boundary; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_River_(Mississippi-Louisiana)" title="Pearl River (Mississippi-Louisiana)">Pearl</a>, the eastern boundary; the <a href="/wiki/Calcasieu_River" title="Calcasieu River">Calcasieu</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ʃ/: 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span>), the <a href="/wiki/Mermentau_River" title="Mermentau River">Mermentau</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vermilion_River_(Louisiana)" title="Vermilion River (Louisiana)">Vermilion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bayou_Teche" title="Bayou Teche">Bayou Teche</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/tʃ/: 'ch' in 'China'">tʃ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>), the <a href="/wiki/Boeuf_River" title="Boeuf River">Boeuf</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span></span>/</a></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Bayou_Lafourche" title="Bayou Lafourche">Bayou Lafourche</a>, the Courtableau River, Bayou D'Arbonne, the Macon River, the <a href="/wiki/Tensas_River" title="Tensas River">Tensas</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span></span>/</a></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Amite_River" title="Amite River">Amite River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tchefuncte_River" title="Tchefuncte River">Tchefuncte</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/tʃ/: 'ch' in 'China'">tʃ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>), the <a href="/wiki/Tickfaw_River" title="Tickfaw River">Tickfaw</a>, the Natalbany River, and a number of other smaller streams, constituting a natural system of navigable waterways, aggregating over 4,000 miles (6,400 km) long.</p>
<p>The state also has political jurisdiction over the approximately 3-mile (4.8 km)-wide portion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsea" title="Subsea">subsea</a> land of the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Continental_Shelf" title="Outer Continental Shelf">inner continental shelf</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>. Through a peculiarity of the <a href="/wiki/Political_geography" title="Political geography">political geography</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, this is substantially less than the 9-mile (14 km)-wide jurisdiction of nearby <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">states</a> <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, which, like Louisiana, have extensive Gulf coastlines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tulanian2008_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tulanian2008-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The southern coast of Louisiana in the United States is among the fastest-disappearing areas in the world. This has largely resulted from human mismanagement of the coast (see <a href="/wiki/Wetlands_of_Louisiana" title="Wetlands of Louisiana">Wetlands of Louisiana</a>). At one time, the land was added to when spring floods from the Mississippi River added sediment and stimulated marsh growth; the land is now shrinking. There are multiple causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddy_2010_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keddy_2010-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Artificial levees block spring flood water that would bring fresh water and sediment to marshes. Swamps have been extensively logged, leaving canals and ditches that allow saline water to move inland. Canals dug for the oil and gas industry also allow storms to move sea water inland, where it damages swamps and marshes. Rising sea waters have exacerbated the problem. Some researchers estimate that the state is losing a land mass equivalent to 30 football fields every day. There are many proposals to save coastal areas by reducing human damage, including restoring natural floods from the Mississippi. Without such restoration, coastal communities will continue to disappear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> And as the communities disappear, more and more people are leaving the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tidwell.282003.29_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tidwell.282003.29-26">[25]</a></sup> Since the coastal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetlands" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a> support an economically important coastal fishery, the loss of wetlands is adversely affecting this industry.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<td colspan="12" style="padding: 2px; text-align: left; line-height: 1.5em; color: red">Average max. and min. temperatures in °C</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="12" style="padding: 2px; text-align: left; line-height: 1.5em; color: blue">Precipitation totals in mm</td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>

</table>
<p>Louisiana has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>Cfa</i>). It has long, hot, humid summers and short, mild winters. The subtropical characteristics of the state are due in large part to the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, which at its farthest point is no more than 200 miles (320 km) away.</p>
<p>Rain is frequent throughout the year, although the summer is slightly wetter than the rest of the year. There is a dip in precipitation in October. Southern Louisiana receives far more copious rainfall, especially during the winter months. Summers in Louisiana have high temperatures from mid-June to mid-September averaging 90 °F (32 °C) or more, and overnight lows averaging above 70 °F (22 °C).</p>
<p>In the summer, the extreme maximum temperature is much warmer in the north than in the south, with temperatures near the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> occasionally reaching 100 °F (38 °C), although temperatures above 95 °F (35 °C) are commonplace. In northern Louisiana, the temperatures reach above 105 °F (41 °C) in the summer.</p>
<p>Temperatures are generally mildly warm in the winter in the southern part of the state, with highs around New Orleans, Baton Rouge, the rest of south Louisiana, and the Gulf of Mexico averaging 66 °F (19 °C). The northern part of the state is mildly cool in the winter, with highs averaging 59 °F (15 °C). The overnight lows in the winter average well above freezing throughout the state, with 46 °F (8 °C) the average near the Gulf and an average low of 37 °F (3 °C) in the winter in the northern part of the state.</p>
<p>Louisiana gets some cold fronts, which frequently drop the temperatures below 20 °F (−8 °C) in the northern part of the state, but almost never do so in the southern part of the state. Snow is not very common near the Gulf of Mexico, although residents in the northern parts of the state can expect one to three snowfalls per year, with the frequency increasing northwards. Louisiana's highest recorded temperature is 114 °F (46 °C) in <a href="/wiki/Plain_Dealing,_Louisiana" title="Plain Dealing, Louisiana">Plain Dealing</a> on August 10, 1936, while the coldest recorded temperature is −16 °F (−27 °C) at <a href="/wiki/Minden,_Louisiana" title="Minden, Louisiana">Minden</a> on February 13, 1899.</p>
<p>Louisiana is often affected by <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a> and is very vulnerable to strikes by major <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_hurricane" title="Atlantic hurricane">hurricanes</a>, particularly the lowlands around and in the New Orleans area. The unique geography of the region, with the many bayous, marshes and inlets, can result in water damage across a wide area from major hurricanes. The area is also prone to frequent thunderstorms, especially in the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Annual_average_number_of_tornadoes_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Annual_average_number_of_tornadoes-28">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The entire state averages over 60 days of thunderstorms a year, more than any other state except <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. Louisiana averages 27 <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a> annually. The entire state is vulnerable to a tornado strike, with the extreme southern portion of the state slightly less so than the rest of the state. Tornadoes are more common from January to March in the southern part of the state, and from February through March in the northern part of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Annual_average_number_of_tornadoes_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Annual_average_number_of_tornadoes-28">[27]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricanes_since_1950">Hurricanes since 1950</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hurricanes since 1950">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>August 28–29, 2012, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Isaac_(2012)" title="Hurricane Isaac (2012)">Isaac</a> (Category 1 at landfall) hits southeast Louisiana 7 years after Katrina (2005).</li>
<li>September 1, 2008, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Gustav" title="Hurricane Gustav">Gustav</a> (Category 2 at landfall) made landfall along the coast near <a href="/wiki/Cocodrie,_Louisiana" title="Cocodrie, Louisiana">Cocodrie</a> in southeastern Louisiana. As late as August 31 it had been projected by the <a href="/wiki/National_Hurricane_Center" title="National Hurricane Center">National Hurricane Center</a> that the hurricane would remain at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_Hurricane_Scale" title="Saffir–Simpson Hurricane Scale">Category 3</a> or above on September 1, but in the event the center of Gustav made landfall as a strong Category 2 hurricane (1 mph below Category 3), and dropped to Category 1 soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> As a result of NHC's forecasts, a massive evacuation of New Orleans took place after many residents having failed to leave for Katrina in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> A significant number of deaths were caused by or attributed to Gustav.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KTBS_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-KTBS-34">[33]</a></sup> Around 1.5 million people were without power in Louisiana on September 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>September 24, 2005, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Rita" title="Hurricane Rita">Rita</a> (Category 3 at landfall) struck southwestern Louisiana, flooding many parishes and cities along the coast, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cameron_Parish" title="Cameron Parish">Cameron Parish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Charles,_Louisiana" title="Lake Charles, Louisiana">Lake Charles</a>, and other towns. The storm's winds weakened the damaged levees in New Orleans and caused renewed flooding in parts of the city.</li>
<li>August 29, 2005, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Katrina</a> (Category 3 at landfall)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> struck and devastated southeastern Louisiana, where it breached and undermined levees in New Orleans, causing 80% of the city to flood. Most people had been evacuated, but the majority of the population became homeless. The city was virtually closed until October. It is estimated that more than two million people in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="Gulf Coast of the United States">Gulf region</a> were displaced by the hurricane, and that more than 1,500 fatalities resulted in Louisiana alone. A public outcry criticized governments at the local, state, and federal levels, for lack of preparation and slowness of response. Louisiana residents relocated across the country for temporary housing, and many have not returned.</li>
</ul>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina_effects_by_region#Louisiana" title="Hurricane Katrina effects by region">Hurricane Katrina effects by region § Louisiana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_Hurricane_Katrina_in_New_Orleans" title="Effects of Hurricane Katrina in New Orleans">Effects of Hurricane Katrina in New Orleans</a></div>
<ul>
<li>October 3, 2002, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Lili" title="Hurricane Lili">Lili</a> (Category 1 at landfall)</li>
<li>August 1992, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Andrew" title="Hurricane Andrew">Andrew</a> (Category 3 at landfall) struck south-central Louisiana. It killed four people; knocked out power to nearly 150,000 citizens; and destroyed crops worth hundreds of millions of dollars.</li>
<li>August 1969, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Camille" title="Hurricane Camille">Camille</a> (Category 5) caused a 23.4 ft (7.1 m) storm surge and killed 250 people. Although Camille officially made landfall in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> and the worst damage occurred there, it also had effects in Louisiana. New Orleans remained dry, with the exception of mild rain-generated flooding in the most low-lying areas.</li>
<li>September 9, 1965, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Betsy" title="Hurricane Betsy">Betsy</a> (Category 3 at landfall) came ashore in Louisiana, causing massive destruction as the first hurricane in history to cause one billion dollars in damage (over ten billion in inflation-adjusted USD). The storm hit New Orleans and flooded nearly 35% of the city (including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_9th_Ward" title="Lower 9th Ward">Lower 9th Ward</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gentilly,_New_Orleans" title="Gentilly, New Orleans">Gentilly</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Mid-City_New_Orleans" title="Mid-City New Orleans">Mid-City</a>). The death toll in the state was 76.</li>
<li>June 1957, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Audrey" title="Hurricane Audrey">Audrey</a> (Category 4) devastated southwest Louisiana, destroying or severely damaging 60–80 percent of the homes and businesses from <a href="/wiki/Cameron,_Louisiana" title="Cameron, Louisiana">Cameron</a> to Grand Chenier. 40,000 people were left homeless and more than 300 people in the state died.</li>
<li>August 15–17, 1915: A hurricane made landfall just west of Galveston. Gales howled throughout Cameron and Vermilion Parishes and as far east as Mobile. Produced tides of 11 feet at Cameron (called Leesburg at the time), 10 feet at Grand Cheniere, and 9.5 feet at Marsh Island; Grand Isle reported water 6 feet deep across the city. The light keeper at the Sabine Pass lighthouse had to turn the lens by hand, as vibrations caused by the wave action put the clockwork out of order. At Sabine Bank, 17 miles offshore the Mouth of the Sabine, damage was noted. Damage estimates for Louisiana and Texas totaled around $50 million. &lt;<a class="external free" href="http://www.thecajuns.com/lahurricanes.htm#August_15-17th,_1915" rel="nofollow">http://www.thecajuns.com/lahurricanes.htm#August_15-17th,_1915</a>&gt;</li>
</ul>
<p>1915 over 300 people drowned below Montegut – four can be identified as white, none of the others have been identified and are assumed to be Indians. The Indian settlement was about 10 miles below Montegut, called by the Indians – Taire-bonne – is now in swamp and can only be reached by boat. This hurricane caused the survivors to move to higher ground.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government-owned_land">Government-owned land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Government-owned land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Owing to its location and geology, the state has high biological diversity. Some vital areas, such as southwestern prairie, have experienced a loss in excess of 98 percent. The pine flatwoods are also at great risk, mostly from fire suppression and urban sprawl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddy_2008_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Keddy_2008-11">[10]</a></sup> There is not yet a properly organized system of natural areas to represent and protect Louisiana's biological diversity. Such a system would consist of a protected system of core areas linked by biological corridors, such as Florida is planning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Louisiana contains a number of areas which, to varying degrees, prevent people from using them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> In addition to <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> areas and a <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Forest" title="United States National Forest">United States National Forest</a>, Louisiana operates a system of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Louisiana_state_parks" title="List of Louisiana state parks">state parks</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Louisiana_state_historic_sites" title="List of Louisiana state historic sites">state historic sites</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_State_Arboretum" title="Louisiana State Arboretum">state preservation area</a>, one <a href="/wiki/Alexander_State_Forest" title="Alexander State Forest">state forest</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_Management_Area" title="Wildlife Management Area">Wildlife Management Areas</a>.</p>
<p>One of Louisiana's largest government-owned areas is Kisatchie National Forest. It is some 600,000 acres in area, more than half of which is <a href="/wiki/Flatwoods" title="Flatwoods">flatwoods</a> vegetation, which supports many rare plant and animal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddy_2008_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Keddy_2008-11">[10]</a></sup> These include the Louisiana pine snake and Red-cockaded woodpecker. The system of government-owned cypress swamps around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a> is another large area, with southern <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> species including egrets, alligators, and sturgeon. At least 12 core areas would be needed to build a "protected areas system" for the state; these would range from southwestern prairies, to the Pearl River Floodplain in the east, to the Mississippi River alluvial swamps in the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keddy_2008_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Keddy_2008-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Park_Service">National Park Service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: National Park Service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Historic or scenic areas managed, protected, or otherwise recognized by the National Park Service include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_National_Heritage_Area" title="Atchafalaya National Heritage Area">Atchafalaya National Heritage Area</a> in Ascension Parish;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cane_River_National_Heritage_Area" title="Cane River National Heritage Area">Cane River National Heritage Area</a> near Natchitoches;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cane_River_Creole_National_Historical_Park" title="Cane River Creole National Historical Park">Cane River Creole National Historical Park</a> near Natchitoches;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jean_Lafitte_National_Historical_Park_and_Preserve" title="Jean Lafitte National Historical Park and Preserve">Jean Lafitte National Historical Park and Preserve</a>, headquartered in New Orleans, with units in St. Bernard Parish, Barataria (Crown Point), and Acadiana (Lafayette);</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poverty_Point_National_Monument" title="Poverty Point National Monument">Poverty Point National Monument</a> at Epps, Louisiana; and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saline_Bayou" title="Saline Bayou">Saline Bayou</a>, a designated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Wild_and_Scenic_River" title="National Wild and Scenic River">National Wild and Scenic River</a> near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winn_Parish" title="Winn Parish">Winn Parish</a> in northern Louisiana.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_Forest_Service">US Forest Service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: US Forest Service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kisatchie_National_Forest" title="Kisatchie National Forest">Kisatchie National Forest</a> is Louisiana's only national forest. It includes 600,000 acres in central and north Louisiana with large areas of flatwoods and longleaf pine forest.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_parks_and_recreational_areas">State parks and recreational areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: State parks and recreational areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Louisiana_state_parks" title="List of Louisiana state parks">List of Louisiana state parks</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Louisiana_state_historic_sites" title="List of Louisiana state historic sites">List of Louisiana state historic sites</a></div>
<p>Louisiana operates a system of 22 state parks, 17 state historic sites and one state <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_State_Arboretum" title="Louisiana State Arboretum">preservation area</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife_management_areas">Wildlife management areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Wildlife management areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Louisiana has 955,973 acres, in four <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregions</a> under the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Louisiana_Wildlife_Management_Areas" title="List of Louisiana Wildlife Management Areas">wildlife management</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Department_of_Wildlife_and_Fisheries" title="Louisiana Department of Wildlife and Fisheries">Louisiana Department of Wildlife and Fisheriess</a>. The <a class="external text" href="http://www.nature.org/ourinitiatives/regions/northamerica/unitedstates/louisiana/index.htm" rel="nofollow">Nature Conservancy</a> also owns and manages a set of natural areas.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_and_Scenic_Rivers">Natural and Scenic Rivers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Natural and Scenic Rivers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Louisiana Natural and Scenic Rivers System provides a degree of protection for 51 rivers, streams and bayous in the state. It is administered by the Louisiana Department of Wildlife and Fisheries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Department_of_Transportation_and_Development" title="Louisiana Department of Transportation and Development">Louisiana Department of Transportation and Development</a> is the state government organization in charge of maintaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_transportation" title="Public transportation">public transportation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Road" title="Road">roadways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bridge" title="Bridge">bridges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>, select <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levees</a>, floodplain management, <a href="/wiki/Port" title="Port">port</a> facilities, commercial vehicles, and <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a> which includes 69 airports.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_numbered_highways_in_Louisiana" title="List of numbered highways in Louisiana">List of numbered highways in Louisiana</a></div>

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Intracoastal_Waterway" title="Intracoastal Waterway">Intracoastal Waterway</a> is an important means of transporting commercial goods such as petroleum and petroleum products, agricultural produce, building materials and manufactured goods.</p>
<p>In 2011, Louisiana ranked among the five deadliest states for debris/litter-caused vehicle accidents per total number of registered vehicles and population size. Figures derived from<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> the NTSHA show at least 25 persons in Louisiana were killed per year in motor vehicle collisions with non-fixed objects, including debris, dumped litter, animals and their carcasses.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Louisiana" title="History of Louisiana">History of Louisiana</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-colonial_history">Pre-colonial history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pre-colonial history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Louisiana was inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> for many millennia before the arrival of Europeans in the 16th century. During the Middle <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_period_in_the_Americas" title="Archaic period in the Americas">Archaic period</a>, Louisiana was the site of the earliest mound complex in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and one of the earliest dated, complex constructions in the Americas, the <a href="/wiki/Watson_Brake" title="Watson Brake">Watson Brake</a> site near present-day <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Louisiana" title="Monroe, Louisiana">Monroe</a>. An 11-mound complex, it was built about 5400 BP (3500 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> The Middle Archaic sites of Caney and Frenchman's Bend have also been securely dated to 5600–5000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>, demonstrating that seasonal hunter-gatherers organized to build complex constructions in present-day northern Louisiana. The Hedgepeth Site in <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Lincoln Parish, Louisiana">Lincoln Parish</a> is more recent, dated to 5200–4500 BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nearly 2,000 years later, <a href="/wiki/Poverty_Point" title="Poverty Point">Poverty Point</a>, the largest and best-known Late Archaic site in the state, was built. Modern-day <a href="/wiki/Epps,_Louisiana" title="Epps, Louisiana">Epps</a> developed near it. The <a href="/wiki/Poverty_Point_culture" title="Poverty Point culture">Poverty Point culture</a> may have reached its peak around 1500 BCE, making it the first complex culture, and possibly the first tribal culture in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> It lasted until approximately 700 BCE.</p>
<p>The Poverty Point culture was followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tchefuncte_site#Tchefuncte_culture" title="Tchefuncte site">Tchefuncte</a> and Lake Cormorant cultures of the Tchula period, local manifestations of Early <a href="/wiki/Woodland_period" title="Woodland period">Woodland period</a>. The Tchefuncte culture were the first people in Louisiana to make large amounts of pottery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tchefuncte_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tchefuncte-44">[43]</a></sup> These cultures lasted until 200 CE. The Middle Woodland period starts in Louisiana with the <a href="/wiki/Marksville_culture" title="Marksville culture">Marksville culture</a> in the southern and eastern part of the state<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prehistory_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prehistory-45">[44]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Fourche_Maline_culture" title="Fourche Maline culture">Fourche Maline culture</a> in the northwestern part of the state. The Marksville culture takes its name from the <a href="/wiki/Marksville_Prehistoric_Indian_Site" title="Marksville Prehistoric Indian Site">Marksville Prehistoric Indian Site</a> in <a href="/wiki/Avoyelles_Parish,_Louisiana" title="Avoyelles Parish, Louisiana">Avoyelles Parish</a>.</p>
<p>These cultures were contemporaneous with the <a href="/wiki/Hopewell_tradition" title="Hopewell tradition">Hopewell cultures</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, and participated in the Hopewell Exchange Network. Trade with peoples to the southwest brought the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_(weapon)" title="Bow (weapon)">bow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arrow" title="Arrow">arrow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OASPAST_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-OASPAST-46">[45]</a></sup> The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burial_mound" title="Burial mound">burial mounds</a> were built at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TejasWoodland_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-TejasWoodland-47">[46]</a></sup> Political power begins to be consolidated as the first <a href="/wiki/Platform_mound" title="Platform mound">platform mounds</a> at ritual centers are constructed for the developing hereditary political and religious leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TejasWoodland_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-TejasWoodland-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 400 CE in the southern part of the state the Late Woodland period had begun with the <a href="/wiki/Baytown_culture" title="Baytown culture">Baytown culture</a>. Population increased dramatically and there is strong evidence of a growing cultural and political complexity. Many Coles Creek sites were erected over earlier Woodland period <a href="/wiki/Morgue" title="Morgue">mortuary</a> mounds. Scholars have speculated that emerging elites were symbolically and physically appropriating dead ancestors to emphasize and project their own authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian period</a> in Louisiana was when <a href="/wiki/Plaquemine_culture" title="Plaquemine culture">Plaquemine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caddoan_Mississippian_culture" title="Caddoan Mississippian culture">Caddoan Mississippian cultures</a> developed and extensive <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> agriculture was adopted. The Plaquemine culture in the lower <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> Valley in western Mississippi and eastern Louisiana began in 1200 CE and continued to about 1400 CE. Good examples of this culture are the <a href="/wiki/Medora_Site" title="Medora Site">Medora Site</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Baton_Rouge_Parish,_Louisiana" title="West Baton Rouge Parish, Louisiana">West Baton Rouge Parish</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Emerald_Mound_Site" title="Emerald Mound Site">Emerald Mound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Winterville_Site" title="Winterville Site">Winterville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holly_Bluff_Site" title="Holly Bluff Site">Holly Bluff</a> sites in Mississippi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plaquemine culture was contemporaneous with the Middle Mississippian culture represented by the primary settlement, the <a href="/wiki/Cahokia" title="Cahokia">Cahokia</a> site near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>. This group is considered ancestral to the <a href="/wiki/Natchez_people" title="Natchez people">Natchez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taensa" title="Taensa">Taensa</a> Peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Plaquemine_Culture.2C_A.D_1000_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Plaquemine_Culture.2C_A.D_1000-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1000 CE in the northwestern part of the state, the Fourche Maline culture had evolved into the Caddoan Mississippian culture. The Caddoan Mississippians covered a large territory, including what is now eastern Oklahoma, western Arkansas, northeast <a href="/wiki/East_Texas" title="East Texas">Texas</a>, and northwest Louisiana. Archeological evidence has demonstrated that the cultural continuity is unbroken from prehistory to the present. The <a href="/wiki/Caddo" title="Caddo">Caddo</a> and related <a href="/wiki/Caddoan_languages" title="Caddoan languages">Caddo language</a> speakers in prehistoric times and at first European contact were the direct ancestors of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caddo_Nation_of_Oklahoma" title="Caddo Nation of Oklahoma">Caddo Nation of Oklahoma</a> of today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TejasLinguistics_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-TejasLinguistics-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many current place names in the state, including <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_River" title="Atchafalaya River">Atchafalaya</a>, Natchitouches (now spelled <a href="/wiki/Natchitoches,_Louisiana" title="Natchitoches, Louisiana">Natchitoches</a>), Caddo, <a href="/wiki/Houma,_Louisiana" title="Houma, Louisiana">Houma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tangipahoa" title="Tangipahoa">Tangipahoa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Avoyel" title="Avoyel">Avoyel</a> (as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avoyelles" title="Avoyelles">Avoyelles</a>), are transliterations of those used in various Native American languages.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration_and_colonization_by_Europeans">Exploration and colonization by Europeans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Exploration and colonization by Europeans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/French_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="French colonization of the Americas">French colonization of the Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_regions_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4515" data-file-width="5018" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Louisiana_regions_map.svg/220px-Louisiana_regions_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Louisiana_regions_map.svg/330px-Louisiana_regions_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Louisiana_regions_map.svg/440px-Louisiana_regions_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_regions_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Louisiana regions</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first European explorers to visit Louisiana came in 1528 when a <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spanish</a> expedition led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panfilo_de_Narv%C3%A1ez" title="Panfilo de Narváez">Panfilo de Narváez</a> located the mouth of the Mississippi River. In 1542, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto_(explorer)" title="Hernando de Soto (explorer)">Hernando de Soto</a>'s expedition skirted to the north and west of the state (encountering Caddo and Tunica groups) and then followed the Mississippi River down to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> in 1543. Spanish interest in Louisiana then faded away for a century and a half.</p>
<p>In the late 17th century, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> and French Canadian expeditions, which included sovereign, religious and commercial aims, established a foothold on the Mississippi River and Gulf Coast. With its first settlements, France laid claim to a vast region of North America and set out to establish a commercial empire and French nation stretching from the Gulf of Mexico to Canada.</p>
<p>In 1682, the French explorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Cavelier_de_La_Salle" title="Robert Cavelier de La Salle">Robert Cavelier de La Salle</a> named the region Louisiana to honor <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">France's King Louis XIV</a>. The first permanent settlement, Fort Maurepas (at what is now <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Springs,_Mississippi" title="Ocean Springs, Mississippi">Ocean Springs, Mississippi</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Biloxi,_Mississippi" title="Biloxi, Mississippi">Biloxi</a>), was founded by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Le_Moyne_d%27Iberville" title="Pierre Le Moyne d'Iberville">Pierre Le Moyne d'Iberville</a>, a French military officer from Canada, in 1699. By then the French had also built a small fort at the mouth of the Mississippi at a settlement they named <a href="/wiki/La_Balize,_Louisiana" title="La Balize, Louisiana">La Balise (or La Balize)</a>, "seamark" in French. By 1721 they built a 62-foot (19 m) wooden lighthouse-type structure to guide ships on the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>A royal ordinance of 1722—following the transfer of the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Country" title="Illinois Country">Illinois Country</a>'s governance from Canada to Louisiana—may have featured the broadest definition of Louisiana: all land claimed by France south of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_Mountains" title="Allegheny Mountains">Alleghenies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ekberg-French_Roots_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ekberg-French_Roots-53">[52]</a></sup> A generation later, trade conflicts between Canada and Louisiana led to a more defined boundary between the French colonies; in 1745, Louisiana governor general <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Rigaud,_Marquis_de_Vaudreuil-Cavagnial" title="Pierre de Rigaud, Marquis de Vaudreuil-Cavagnial">Vaudreuil</a> set the northern and eastern bounds of his domain as the <a href="/wiki/Wabash_River" title="Wabash River">Wabash</a> valley up to the mouth of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vermilion_River_(Wabash_River_tributary)" title="Vermilion River (Wabash River tributary)">Vermilion River</a> (near present-day <a href="/wiki/Danville,_Illinois" title="Danville, Illinois">Danville, Illinois</a>); from there, northwest to <a href="/wiki/Starved_Rock_State_Park" title="Starved Rock State Park"><i>le Rocher</i></a> on the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_River" title="Illinois River">Illinois River</a>, and from there west to the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Rock_River_(Mississippi_River)" title="Rock River (Mississippi River)">Rock River</a> (at present day <a href="/wiki/Rock_Island,_Illinois" title="Rock Island, Illinois">Rock Island, Illinois</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ekberg-French_Roots_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ekberg-French_Roots-53">[52]</a></sup> Thus, <a href="/wiki/Vincennes,_Indiana" title="Vincennes, Indiana">Vincennes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peoria,_Illinois" title="Peoria, Illinois">Peoria</a> were the limit of Louisiana'a reach; the outposts at <a href="/wiki/Ouiatenon" title="Ouiatenon">Ouiatenon</a> (on the upper Wabash near present-day <a href="/wiki/Lafayette,_Indiana" title="Lafayette, Indiana">Lafayette, Indiana</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Miamis" title="Fort Miamis">Fort Miamis</a> (near present-day <a href="/wiki/Fort_Wayne,_Indiana" title="Fort Wayne, Indiana">Fort Wayne, Indiana</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prairie_du_Chien" title="Prairie du Chien">Prairie du Chien</a> operated as dependencies of Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ekberg-French_Roots_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ekberg-French_Roots-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The settlement of <a href="/wiki/Natchitoches,_Louisiana" title="Natchitoches, Louisiana">Natchitoches</a> (along the Red River in present-day northwest Louisiana) was established in 1714 by Louis Juchereau de St. Denis, making it the oldest permanent European settlement in the modern state of Louisiana. The French settlement had two purposes: to establish trade with the Spanish in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> via the Old San Antonio Road, and to deter Spanish advances into Louisiana. The settlement soon became a flourishing river port and crossroads, giving rise to vast cotton kingdoms along the river. Over time, planters developed large plantations and built fine homes in a growing town. This became a pattern repeated in New Orleans and other places.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1001" data-file-width="1500" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg/220px-Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg/330px-Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg/440px-Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atchafalaya_Basin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
French Acadians, who came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Cajuns" title="Cajuns">Cajuns</a>, settled the swamps of southern Louisiana, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Atchafalaya_Basin" title="Atchafalaya Basin">Atchafalaya Basin</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Louisiana's French settlements contributed to further exploration and outposts, concentrated along the banks of the Mississippi and its major tributaries, from Louisiana to as far north as the region called the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Country" title="Illinois Country">Illinois Country</a>, around present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>.</p>
<p>Initially, <a href="/wiki/Mobile,_Alabama" title="Mobile, Alabama">Mobile</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Biloxi,_Mississippi" title="Biloxi, Mississippi">Biloxi</a> functioned as the capital of the colony. Recognizing the importance of the Mississippi River to trade and military interests, France made New Orleans the seat of civilian and military authority south of the Great Lakes in 1722. From then until the United States acquired the territory in the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> of 1803, France and Spain jockeyed for control of New Orleans and the lands west of the Mississippi.</p>
<p>In the 1720s, German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immigrants" title="Immigrants">immigrants</a> settled along the Mississippi River, in a region referred to as the <a href="/wiki/German_Coast" title="German Coast">German Coast</a>.</p>
<p>France ceded most of its territory to the east of the Mississippi to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> in 1763, in the aftermath of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Britain_in_the_Seven_Years_War" title="Great Britain in the Seven Years War">Britain's victory in the Seven Years' War</a> (generally referred to in North America as the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>). The rest of Louisiana, including the area around New Orleans and the parishes around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pontchartrain" title="Lake Pontchartrain">Lake Pontchartrain</a>, had become a colony of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Fontainebleau_(1762)" title="Treaty of Fontainebleau (1762)">Treaty of Fontainebleau</a> (1762), although the transfer of power on either side of the river would be delayed until later in the decade.</p>
<p>In 1765, during Spanish rule, several thousand French-speaking refugees from the region of <a href="/wiki/Acadia" title="Acadia">Acadia</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>) made their way to Louisiana after having been <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_the_Acadians" title="Expulsion of the Acadians">expelled</a> from their homelands by the British during the French and Indian War. They settled chiefly in the southwestern Louisiana region now called <a href="/wiki/Acadiana" title="Acadiana">Acadiana</a>. The Spanish, eager to gain more Catholic settlers, welcomed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acadian" title="Acadian">Acadian</a> refugees, the ancestors of Louisiana's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cajun" title="Cajun">Cajuns</a>.</p>
<p>Spanish Canary Islanders, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isle%C3%B1os" title="Isleños">Isleños</a>, emigrated from the Canary Islands of Spain to Louisiana under the Spanish crown between 1778 and 1783.</p>
<p>In 1800, France's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> reacquired Louisiana from Spain in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Treaty_of_San_Ildefonso" title="Third Treaty of San Ildefonso">Treaty of San Ildefonso</a>, an arrangement kept secret for two years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_slavery">Expansion of slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Expansion of slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_Louisiana" title="History of slavery in Louisiana">History of slavery in Louisiana</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Le_Moyne,_Sieur_de_Bienville" title="Jean-Baptiste Le Moyne, Sieur de Bienville">Jean-Baptiste Le Moyne, Sieur de Bienville</a> brought the first two African slaves to Louisiana in 1708, transporting them from a French colony in the West Indies. In 1709, French financier <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Crozat" title="Antoine Crozat">Antoine Crozat</a> obtained a monopoly of commerce in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Louisiane" title="La Louisiane">La Louisiane</a>, which extended from the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> to what is now <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>. "That concession allowed him to bring in a cargo of blacks from <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> every year," the British historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_Thomas_(writer)" title="Hugh Thomas (writer)">Hugh Thomas</a> wrote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Physical conditions, including disease, were so harsh there was high mortality among both the colonists and the slaves, resulting in continuing demand and importation of slaves.</p>
<p>Starting in 1719, traders began to import slaves in higher numbers; two French ships, the <i>Du Maine</i> and the <i>Aurore</i>, arrived in New Orleans carrying more than 500 black slaves coming from Africa. Previous slaves in Louisiana had been transported from French colonies in the West Indies. By the end of 1721, New Orleans counted 1256 inhabitants, of whom about half were slaves.</p>
<p>In 1724, the French government issued a law called the <a href="/wiki/Code_Noir" title="Code Noir">Code Noir</a> ("Black Code" in English) which "regulate[d] the interaction of whites [blancs] and blacks [noirs] in its colony of Louisiana<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> (which was much larger than the current state of Louisiana). The law consisted of fifty-seven articles which regulated religion in the colony, outlawed "interracial" marriages (those between people of different skin color, the varying shades of which were also defined by law), restricted manumission, outlined legal punishment of slaves for various offenses, and defined some obligations of owners to their slaves. The main intent of the French government was to assert control over the slave system of agriculture in Louisiana and to impose restrictions on slaveowners there. In practice, the Code Noir was exceedingly difficult to enforce from afar. Some priests continued to perform interracial marriage ceremonies, for example, and some slaveholders continued to manumit slaves without permission while others punished slaves brutally.</p>
<p>Article II of the Code Noir of 1724 required owners to provide their slaves with religious education in the state religion, Roman Catholicism. Sunday was to be a day of rest for slaves. On days off, slaves were expected to feed and take care of themselves. During the 1740s economic crisis in the colony, owners had trouble feeding their slaves and themselves. Giving them time off also effectively gave more power to slaves, who started cultivating their own gardens and crafting items for sale as their own property. They began to participate in the economic development of the colony while at the same time increasing independence and self-subsistence.</p>
<p>Article VI of the Code Noir forbade mixed marriages, forbade but did little to protect slave women from rape by their owners, overseers or other slaves. On balance, the Code benefitted the owners but had more protections and flexibility than did the institution of slavery in the southern <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a>.</p>
<p>The Louisiana Black Code of 1806 made the cruel punishment of slaves a crime, but owners and overseers were seldom prosecuted for such acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fugitive slaves, called maroons, could easily hide in the backcountry of the bayous and survive in small settlements. The word "maroon" comes from the French "marron," it means feral or fugitive.</p>
<p>In the late 18th century, the last Spanish governor of the Louisiana territory wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Truly, it is impossible for lower Louisiana to get along without slaves and with the use of slaves, the colony had been making great strides toward prosperity and wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Slave_Trade_p._548_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Slave_Trade_p._548-57">[56]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>When the United States <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">purchased Louisiana</a> in 1803, it was soon accepted that enslaved Africans could be brought to Louisiana as easily as they were brought to neighboring <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, though it violated U.S. law to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Slave_Trade_p._548_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Slave_Trade_p._548-57">[56]</a></sup> Despite demands by <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> Rep. <a href="/wiki/James_Hillhouse" title="James Hillhouse">James Hillhouse</a> and by the pamphleteer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a> to enforce existing federal law against slavery in the newly acquired territory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Slave_Trade_p._548_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Slave_Trade_p._548-57">[56]</a></sup> slavery prevailed because it was the source of great profits and the lowest-cost labor.</p>
<p>At the start of the 19th century, Louisiana was a small producer of sugar with a relatively small number of slaves, compared to Saint-Domingue and the West Indies. It soon thereafter became a major sugar producer as new settlers arrived to develop plantations. <a href="/wiki/William_C._C._Claiborne" title="William C. C. Claiborne">William C. C. Claiborne</a>, Louisiana's first United States governor, said that African slave labor was needed because white laborers "cannot be had in this unhealthy climate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Hugh Thomas wrote that Claiborne was unable to enforce the abolition of the African slave trade, which the US and Great Britain adopted in 1808. The United States continued to protect the domestic slave trade, including the coastwise trade – the transport of slaves by ship along the Atlantic Coast and to New Orleans and other Gulf ports.</p>
<p>By 1840, New Orleans had the biggest slave market in the United States, which contributed greatly to the economy of the city and of the state. New Orleans had become one of the wealthiest cities, and the third largest city, in the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> The ban on the African slave trade and importation of slaves had increased demand in the domestic market. During the decades after the American Revolutionary War, more than one million enslaved African Americans underwent forced migration from the Upper South to the Deep South, two thirds of them in the slave trade. Others were transported by their owners as slaveholders moved west for new lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>With changing agriculture in the Upper South as planters shifted from tobacco to less labor-intensive mixed agriculture, planters had excess laborers. Many sold slaves to traders to take to the Deep South. Slaves were driven by traders overland from the Upper South or transported to New Orleans and other coastal markets by ship in the <a href="/wiki/Coastwise_slave_trade" title="Coastwise slave trade">coastwise slave trade</a>. After sales in New Orleans, steamboats operating on the Mississippi transported slaves upstream to markets or plantation destinations at Natchez and Memphis.</p>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> was developed for cotton and sugar in the first half of the nineteenth century, demand for slaves increased. This resulted in a massive forced migration (through the slave trade) of more than one million African Americans from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_South" title="Upper South">Upper South</a> to the Deep South. Many traders brought slaves to New Orleans for domestic sale, and by 1840, New Orleans had the largest slave market in the country, was the third-largest city, and was one of the wealthiest cities.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Free_Woman_of_Color_with_daughter_NOLA_Collage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1148" data-file-width="947" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Free_Woman_of_Color_with_daughter_NOLA_Collage.jpg/220px-Free_Woman_of_Color_with_daughter_NOLA_Collage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Free_Woman_of_Color_with_daughter_NOLA_Collage.jpg/330px-Free_Woman_of_Color_with_daughter_NOLA_Collage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Free_Woman_of_Color_with_daughter_NOLA_Collage.jpg/440px-Free_Woman_of_Color_with_daughter_NOLA_Collage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
