<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Louisiana_media" title="Category:Louisiana media">Category:Louisiana media</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_school_districts_in_Louisiana" title="List of school districts in Louisiana">List of school districts in Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_Louisiana" title="List of colleges and universities in Louisiana">List of colleges and universities in Louisiana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_immersion#USA" title="French immersion">French immersion in Louisiana</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Science_Education_Act" title="Louisiana Science Education Act">Louisiana Science Education Act</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> is a controversial law passed by the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_State_Legislature" title="Louisiana State Legislature">Louisiana Legislature</a> on June 11, 2008 and signed into law by Governor <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Jindal" title="Bobby Jindal">Bobby Jindal</a> on June 25. The act allows public school teachers to use supplemental materials in the science classroom which are critical of established science on such topics as the theory of evolution and global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dawkins-dvorsky_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-dawkins-dvorsky-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sports_teams_in_Louisiana" title="List of sports teams in Louisiana">List of sports teams in Louisiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sports_in_New_Orleans" title="Sports in New Orleans">Sports in New Orleans</a></div>
<p>Louisiana is nominally the least populous state with more than one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_professional_sports_league" title="Major professional sports league">major professional sports league</a> franchise: the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a>'s <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_Pelicans" title="New Orleans Pelicans">New Orleans Pelicans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a>'s <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_Saints" title="New Orleans Saints">New Orleans Saints</a>. Louisiana has a AAA Minor League baseball team, the <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_Baby_Cakes" title="New Orleans Baby Cakes">New Orleans Baby Cakes</a>. The Baby Cakes are currently affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Miami_Marlins" title="Miami Marlins">Miami Marlins</a>.</p>
<p>Louisiana has 12 collegiate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCAA" title="NCAA">NCAA</a> Division I programs, a high number given its population. The state has no NCAA Division II teams and only two NCAA Division III teams. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSU_Tigers" title="LSU Tigers">LSU Tigers</a> football team has won 11 <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a> titles, six <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Bowl" title="Sugar Bowl">Sugar Bowls</a> and three national championships.</p>
<p>Each year New Orleans plays host to the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_Bowl" title="Sugar Bowl">Sugar Bowl</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_Bowl" title="New Orleans Bowl">New Orleans Bowl</a> college football games, and Shreveport hosts the <a href="/wiki/Independence_Bowl" title="Independence Bowl">Independence Bowl</a>. Also, New Orleans has hosted the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl" title="Super Bowl">Super Bowl</a> a record seven times, as well as the <a href="/wiki/BCS_National_Championship_Game" title="BCS National Championship Game">BCS National Championship Game</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA_All-Star_Game" title="NBA All-Star Game">NBA All-Star Game</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCAA_Men%27s_Division_I_Basketball_Championship" title="NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship">NCAA Men's Division I Basketball Championship</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Zurich_Classic_of_New_Orleans" title="Zurich Classic of New Orleans">Zurich Classic of New Orleans</a>, is a <a href="/wiki/PGA_Tour" title="PGA Tour">PGA Tour</a> golf tournament held since 1938. The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_%E2%80%98n%27_Roll_Mardi_Gras_Marathon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rock ‘n' Roll Mardi Gras Marathon (page does not exist)">Rock ‘n' Roll Mardi Gras Marathon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crescent_City_Classic" title="Crescent City Classic">Crescent City Classic</a> are two road running competitions held at New Orleans.</p>
<p>As of 2016, Louisiana was the birthplace of the most NFL players per capita for the eighth year in a row.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Louisiana" title="Culture of Louisiana">Culture of Louisiana</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CreoleFood.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1321" data-file-width="1631" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/CreoleFood.jpg/220px-CreoleFood.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/CreoleFood.jpg/330px-CreoleFood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/CreoleFood.jpg/440px-CreoleFood.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CreoleFood.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dishes typical of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_cuisine" title="Louisiana Creole cuisine">Louisiana Creole cuisine</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Louisiana is home to many, especially notable are the distinct culture of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creoles" title="Louisiana Creoles">Louisiana Creoles</a>, typically people of color, descendants of free mixed-race families of the colonial and early statehood periods.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African_culture">African culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: African culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The French colony of <i>La Louisiane</i> struggled for decades to survive. Conditions were harsh, the climate and soil were unsuitable for certain crops the colonists knew, and they suffered from regional tropical diseases. Both colonists and the slaves they imported had high mortality rates. The settlers kept importing slaves, which resulted in a high proportion of native Africans from West Africa, who continued to practice their culture in new surroundings. As described by historian <a href="/wiki/Gwendolyn_Midlo_Hall" title="Gwendolyn Midlo Hall">Gwendolyn Midlo Hall</a>, they developed a marked Afro-Creole culture in the colonial era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the turn of the 18th century and in the early 1800s, New Orleans received a major influx of white and mixed-race refugees fleeing the violence of the <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Haitian Revolution</a>, many of whom brought their slaves with them. This added another infusion of African culture to the city, as more slaves in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Domingue" title="Saint-Domingue">Saint-Domingue</a> were from Africa than in the United States. They strongly influenced the African-American culture of the city in terms of dance, music and religious practices.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Louisiana_Creole_culture">Louisiana Creole culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Louisiana Creole culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_people" title="Louisiana Creole people">Creole</a> culture is an amalgamation of French, African, Spanish (and other European), and Native American cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> Creole comes from the Portuguese word <i>crioulo</i>; originally it referred to a colonist of European (specifically French) descent who was born in the New World, in comparison to immigrants from France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> The oldest Louisiana manuscript to use the word "Creole," from 1782, applied it to a slave born in the French colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> But originally it referred more generally to the French colonists born in Louisiana.</p>
<p>Over time, there developed in the French colony a relatively large group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creoles_of_Color" title="Creoles of Color">Creoles of Color</a> (<i>gens de couleur libres</i>), who were primarily descended from African slave women and French men (later other Europeans became part of the mix, as well as some Native Americans.) Often the French would free their concubines and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed-race" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> children, and pass on social capital to them. They might educate sons in France, for instance, and help them enter the French Army for a career. They also settled capital or property on their mistresses and children. The free people of color gained more rights in the colony and sometimes education; they generally spoke French and were Roman Catholic. Many became artisans and property owners. Over time, the term "Creole" became associated with this class of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creoles_of_Color" title="Creoles of Color">Creoles of Color</a>, many of whom achieved freedom long before the Civil War.</p>
<p>Wealthy French Creoles generally maintained town houses in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> as well as houses on their large sugar plantations outside town along the Mississippi River. New Orleans had the largest population of free people of color in the region; they could find work there and created their own culture, marrying among themselves for decades.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acadian_culture">Acadian culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Acadian culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The ancestors of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cajun" title="Cajun">Cajuns</a> immigrated from west central France to New France, where they settled in the Atlantic provinces of <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, known originally as <a href="/wiki/Acadia" title="Acadia">Acadia</a>. After the British defeated France in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> (<a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>) in 1763, France ceded its territory east of the Mississippi River to Britain. The British forcibly separated families and evicted them from Acadia because they refused to vow loyalty to the new British regime. The Acadians were deported to England, New England, and France. Some escaped the British remained in French Canada.</p>
<p>Others scattered, to France, Canada, Mexico, or the Falkland Islands. Many Acadian refugees settled in south Louisiana in the region around <a href="/wiki/Lafayette,_Louisiana" title="Lafayette, Louisiana">Lafayette</a> and the LaFourche Bayou country. They developed a distinct rural culture there that was different from that of the French Creole colonists in the New Orleans area. Intermarrying with others in the area, they developed what was called Cajun music, cuisine and culture. Until the 1970s, the term "Cajun" was considered somewhat derogatory.</p>
<h3><span id="Isleño_culture"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Isle.C3.B1o_culture">Isleño culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Isleño culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A third distinct culture in Louisiana is that of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isle%C3%B1os" title="Isleños">Isleños</a></i>, descendants of Spanish <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islanders</a> who migrated from the Canary Islands of Spain under the Spanish crown beginning in the mid-1770s. They developed four main communities, but many relocated to what is modern-day <a href="/wiki/St._Bernard_Parish,_Louisiana" title="St. Bernard Parish, Louisiana">St. Bernard Parish</a>. This is where the majority of the Isleño population is still concentrated. An annual festival called Fiesta celebrates the heritage of the Isleños.</p>
<p>St Bernard Parish has an Isleños museum, cemetery and church, as well as many street names with Spanish words and Spanish surnames from this heritage. Some members of the Isleño community still speak Spanish – with their own Canary Islander accent. Numerous Isleño identity organizations, and many members of Isleños society keep contact with the Canary Islands of Spain.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="446" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg/220px-Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg/330px-Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg/440px-Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Native_Languages_of_Louisiana.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The languages of historic Native American tribes that occupied what is now Louisiana include: Tunica, Caddo, Natchez, Choctaw, Atakapa, Chitimacha and Houma.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to a 2010 study by the Modern Language Association, among persons five years old and older,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MLA_Data_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-MLA_Data-1">[1]</a></sup> 91.26% of Louisiana residents speak only <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> at home, 3.45% speak <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> (standard French, French Creole, or Cajun French), 3.30% speak <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, and 0.59% speak <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>.</p>
<p>Historically, Native American peoples in the area at the time of European encounter were seven tribes distinguished by their languages: <a href="/wiki/Caddo_language" title="Caddo language">Caddo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunica_language" title="Tunica language">Tunica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natchez_language" title="Natchez language">Natchez</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houma_language" title="Houma language">Houma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choctaw_language" title="Choctaw language">Choctaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atakapa_language" title="Atakapa language">Atakapa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chitimacha_language" title="Chitimacha language">Chitimacha</a>. Of these, only Caddo and Choctaw still have living native speakers, although several other tribes are working to teach and revitalize their languages. Other Native American peoples migrated into the region, escaping from European pressure from the east. Among these were <a href="/wiki/Alabama_language" title="Alabama language">Alabama</a>, Biloxi, Koasati, and Ofo peoples.</p>
<p>Starting in the 1700s, French colonists began to settle along the coast and founded New Orleans. They established French culture and language institutions. They imported thousands of slaves from tribes of West Africa, who spoke several different languages. In the creolization process, the slaves developed a Louisiana Creole dialect incorporating both French and African forms, which colonists adopted to communicate with them, and which persisted beyond slavery. In the 20th century, there were still people of mixed race, particularly, who spoke Louisiana Creole French.</p>
<p>During the 19th century after the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> by the United States, English gradually gained prominence for business and government due to the shift in population with settlement by numerous Americans who were English speakers. Many ethnic French families continued to use French in private. Slaves and some free people of color also spoke Louisiana Creole French. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Louisiana#Louisiana_Constitution_of_1812" title="Constitution of Louisiana">State Constitution of 1812</a> gave English official status in legal proceedings, but use of French remained widespread. Subsequent state constitutions reflect the diminishing importance of French. The 1868 constitution, passed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era" title="Reconstruction era">Reconstruction era</a> before Louisiana was re-admitted to the Union, banned laws requiring the publication of legal proceedings in languages other than English. Subsequently, the legal status of French recovered somewhat, but it never regained its pre-<a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> prominence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several unique dialects of French, Creole, and English are spoken in Louisiana. Dialects of the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French language</a> are: <a href="/wiki/Colonial_French" title="Colonial French">Colonial French</a> and Houma French. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_French" title="Louisiana Creole French">Louisiana Creole French</a> is the term for one of the Creole languages. Two unique dialects developed of the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a>: <a href="/wiki/Cajun_English" title="Cajun English">Louisiana English</a>, a French-influenced variety of English; and what is informally known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yat_(New_Orleans)" title="Yat (New Orleans)">Yat</a>, which resembles the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_English" title="New York City English">New York City dialect</a>, particularly that of historical <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a>. Both accents were influenced by large communities of immigrant <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a>, but the Yat dialect, which developed in New Orleans, was also influenced by French and Spanish.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1920" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg/220px-Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg/330px-Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg/440px-Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signalisation_routi%C3%A8re_bilingue_%C3%A0_l%27entr%C3%A9e_de_la_Louisiane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Louisiana's bilingual state welcome sign, recognizing its French heritage.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Colonial_French" title="Colonial French">Colonial French</a> was the dominant language of white settlers in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">Louisiana</a> during the French colonial period; it was spoken primarily by the French Creoles (native-born). In addition to this dialect, the mixed-race people and slaves developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_French" title="Louisiana Creole French">Louisiana Creole</a>, with a base in West African languages. The limited years of Spanish rule at the end of the 18th century did not result in widespread adoption of the Spanish language. French and Louisiana Creole are still used in modern-day Louisiana, often in family gatherings. English and its associated dialects became predominant after the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> of 1803, after which the area became dominated by numerous English speakers. In some regions, English was influenced by French, as seen with Louisiana English. <a href="/wiki/Colonial_French" title="Colonial French">Colonial French</a>, although mistakenly named Cajun French by some Cajuns, has persisted alongside English.</p>
<p>Renewed interest in the French language in Louisiana has led to the establishment of Canadian-modeled <a href="/wiki/French_immersion" title="French immersion">French immersion</a> schools, as well as bilingual signage in the historic French neighborhoods of New Orleans and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lafayette_(Louisiana)" title="Lafayette (Louisiana)">Lafayette</a>. Organizations such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CODOFIL" title="CODOFIL">CODOFIL</a> promote use of the French language in the state.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Louisiana" title="Literature of Louisiana">Literature of Louisiana</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The largest denominations by number of adherents in 2010 were the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> with 1,200,900; <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> with 709,650; and the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> with 146,848. Non-denominational <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Protestant" title="Evangelical Protestant">Evangelical Protestant</a> congregations had 195,903 members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-132">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>As in other Southern states, the majority of Louisianians belong to various Protestant denominations, with Protestants comprising 57% of the state's adult population. Protestants are concentrated in the northern and central parts of the state and in the northern tier of the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Parishes" title="Florida Parishes">Florida Parishes</a>. Because of French and Spanish heritage, and their descendants the Creoles, and later Irish, Italian, Portuguese and German immigrants, southern Louisiana and the greater New Orleans area are predominantly Catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since Creoles were the first settlers, planters and leaders of the territory, they have traditionally been well represented in politics. For instance, most of the early governors were Creole Catholics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> Because Catholics still constitute a significant fraction of Louisiana's population, they have continued to be influential in state politics. As of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> both Senators and the Governor were Catholic. The high proportion and influence of the Catholic population makes Louisiana distinct among Southern states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> communities are established in the state's larger cities, notably New Orleans and Baton Rouge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> The most significant of these is the Jewish community of the New Orleans area. In 2000, before the 2005 Hurricane Katrina, its population was about 12,000. Louisiana was among the southern states with a significant Jewish population before the 20th century; Virginia, South Carolina, and Georgia also had influential Jewish populations in some of their major cities from the 18th and 19th centuries. The earliest Jewish colonists were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sephardic_Jews" title="Sephardic Jews">Sephardic Jews</a> who immigrated with English colonists from <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. Later in the 19th century, German Jews began to immigrate, followed by those from eastern Europe and the Russian Empire in the late 19th and early 20th centuries.</p>
<p>Prominent Jews in Louisiana's political leadership have included Whig (later Democrat) <a href="/wiki/Judah_P._Benjamin" title="Judah P. Benjamin">Judah P. Benjamin</a> (1811–1884), who represented Louisiana in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" title="U.S. Senate">U.S. Senate</a> before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> and then became the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> Secretary of State; Democrat-turned-Republican <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hahn" title="Michael Hahn">Michael Hahn</a> who was elected as governor, serving 1864–1865 when Louisiana was occupied by the Union Army, and later elected in 1884 as a US Congressman;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Democrat <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Meyer" title="Adolph Meyer">Adolph Meyer</a> (1842–1908), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confederate_Army" title="Confederate Army">Confederate Army</a> officer who represented the state in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._House" title="U.S. House">U.S. House</a> from 1891 until his death in 1908; <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_(U.S._state_government)" title="Secretary of State (U.S. state government)">Secretary of State</a> <a href="/wiki/Jay_Dardenne" title="Jay Dardenne">Jay Dardenne</a> (1954–), and Republican (<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> before 2011) <a href="/wiki/List_of_Attorneys_General_of_Louisiana" title="List of Attorneys General of Louisiana">Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Caldwell" title="Buddy Caldwell">Buddy Caldwell</a> (1946–).</p>

<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.laheritage.org/CreoleHeritage/index.html" rel="nofollow">"French Creole Heritage"</a>. <li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Ward, Roger K (Summer 1997). <a class="external text" href="http://digitalcommons.law.lsu.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=5694&amp;context=lalrev" rel="nofollow">"The French Language in Louisiana Law and Legal Education: A Requiem"</a>. <i>Louisiana Law Review</i>. <b>57</b> (4).</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALouisiana&amp;rft.atitle=The+French+Language+in+Louisiana+Law+and+Legal+Education%3A+A+Requiem&amp;rft.aufirst=Roger+K&amp;rft.aulast=Ward&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.jtitle=Louisiana+Law+Review&amp;rft.ssn=summer&amp;rft.volume=57&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdigitalcommons.law.lsu.edu%2Fcgi%2Fviewcontent.cgi%3Farticle%3D5694%26context%3Dlalrev&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-www.thearda.com-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-www.thearda.com_132-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.thearda.com/rcms2010/r/s/22/rcms2010_22_state_adh_2010.asp" rel="nofollow">"The Association of Religion Data Archives | State Membership Report"</a>. www.thearda.com<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For Louisiana's position in a larger religious context, see <a href="/wiki/Bible_Belt" title="Bible Belt">Bible Belt</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><img alt="Wikisource-logo.svg" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/12px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/18px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/> <cite class="citation encyclopaedia">Herbermann, Charles, ed. (1913). "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia_(1913)/Louisiana" title="wikisource:Catholic Encyclopedia (1913)/Louisiana">Louisiana</a>". <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="Catholic Encyclopedia">Catholic Encyclopedia</a></i>. New York: Robert Appleton Company.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALouisiana&amp;rft.atitle=Louisiana&amp;rft.btitle=Catholic+Encyclopedia&amp;rft.date=1913&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Robert+Appleton+Company&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Other Southern states – such as Maryland and Texas – have longstanding indigenous Catholic populations, and Florida's largely Catholic population of Cuban emigres has been influential since the 1960s. Yet, Louisiana is still unusual or exceptional in its extent of aboriginal Catholic settlement and influence. Among states in the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> (discounting <a href="/wiki/Florida_Panhandle" title="Florida Panhandle">Florida's Panhandle</a> and much of Texas) the historic role of Catholicism in Louisiana is unparalleled and unique. Among the states of the Union, Louisiana's unique use of the term <i>parish</i> (French <i>la parouche</i> or "la paroisse") for <i>county</i> is rooted in the pre-statehood role of Catholic church parishes in the administration of government.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Isaacs, Ronald H. <i>The Jewish Information Source Book: A Dictionary and Almanac</i>, Northvale, NJ: Jason Aronson, Inc., 1993. p. 202.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150712063709/http://tulane.edu/news/newwave/011210_sinai.cfm" rel="nofollow">"Sinai Scholars Seek Students"</a>. Tulane University. January 12, 2010. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://tulane.edu/news/newwave/011210_sinai.cfm" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on July 12, 2015. <q>Registration is open for the spring session of the Sinai Scholars Society, Tulane chapter. The national organization provides funding for a course on Judaism each semester at more than 50 campuses nationwide.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALouisiana&amp;rft.atitle=Sinai+Scholars+Seek+Students&amp;rft.date=2010-01-12&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Ftulane.edu%2Fnews%2Fnewwave%2F011210_sinai.cfm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.knowla.org/entry/940/" rel="nofollow">"Michael Hahn." <i>KnowLA Encyclopedia of Louisiana.</i> Ed. <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><i>The Sugar Masters: Planters and Slaves in Louisiana's Cane World, 1820–1860</i> by Richard Follett, Louisiana State University Press, 2007. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8071-3247-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8071-3247-0">978-0-8071-3247-0</a></li>
<li><i>The Slave Trade: The Story of the Atlantic Slave Trade, 1440–1870</i> by Hugh Thomas. 1997: Simon and Schuster. p. 548.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Inhuman_Bondage:_The_Rise_and_Fall_of_Slavery_in_the_New_World" title="Inhuman Bondage: The Rise and Fall of Slavery in the New World">Inhuman Bondage: The Rise and Fall of Slavery in the New World</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/David_Brion_Davis" title="David Brion Davis">David Brion Davis</a> 2006: Oxford University Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-533944-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-533944-4">978-0-19-533944-4</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiannopoulos,_A.N." title="Yiannopoulos, A.N.">Yiannopoulos, A.N.</a>, <i>The Civil Codes of Louisiana</i> (reprinted from Civil Law System: Louisiana and Comparative law, A Coursebook: Texts, Cases and Materials, 3d Edition; similar to version in preface to Louisiana Civil Code, ed. by Yiannopoulos)</li>
<li>Rodolfo Batiza, "The Louisiana Civil Code of 1808: Its Actual Sources and Present Relevance," 46 <i>TUL. L. REV.</i> 4 (1971); Rodolfo Batiza, "Sources of the Civil Code of 1808, Facts and Speculation: A Rejoinder," 46 <i>TUL. L. REV.</i> 628 (1972); Robert A. Pascal, Sources of the Digest of 1808: A Reply to Professor Batiza, 46 TUL. L. REV. 603 (1972); Joseph M. Sweeney, Tournament of Scholars Over the Sources of the Civil Code of 1808,46 TUL. L. REV. 585 (1972).</li>
<li>The standard history of the state, though only through the Civil War, is <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gayarr%C3%A9" title="Charles Gayarré">Charles Gayarré</a>'s <i>History of Louisiana'</i> (various editions, culminating in 1866, 4 vols., with a posthumous and further expanded edition in 1885).</li>
<li>A number of accounts by 17th- and 18th-century French explorers: Jean-Bernard Bossu, François-Marie Perrin du Lac, Pierre-François-Xavier de Charlevoix, Dumont (as published by Fr. Mascrier), Fr. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Hennepin" title="Louis Hennepin">Louis Hennepin</a>, Lahontan, Louis Narcisse Baudry des Lozières, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_B%C3%A9nard_de_la_Harpe" title="Jean-Baptiste Bénard de la Harpe">Jean-Baptiste Bénard de la Harpe</a>, and Laval. In this group, the explorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antoine_Simon_Le_Page_du_Pratz" title="Antoine Simon Le Page du Pratz">Antoine Simon Le Page du Pratz</a> may be the first historian of Louisiana with his <i>Histoire de la Louisiane</i> (3 vols., Paris, 1758; 2 vols., London, 1763)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Xavier_Martin" title="François Xavier Martin">François Xavier Martin</a>'s <i>History of Louisiana</i> (2 vols., New Orleans, 1827–1829, later ed. by J. F. Condon, continued to 1861, New Orleans, 1882) is the first scholarly treatment of the subject, along with <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Barb%C3%A9-Marbois" title="François Barbé-Marbois">François Barbé-Marbois</a>' <i>Histoire de la Louisiane et de la cession de colonie par la France aux Etats-Unis</i> (Paris, 1829; in English, Philadelphia, 1830).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alc%C3%A9e_Fortier" title="Alcée Fortier">Alcée Fortier</a>'s <i>A History of Louisiana</i> (N.Y., 4 vols., 1904) is the most recent of the large-scale scholarly histories of the state.</li>
<li>The official works of Albert Phelps and <a href="/wiki/Grace_King" title="Grace King">Grace King</a>, the publications of the Louisiana Historical Society and several works on <a href="/wiki/History_of_New_Orleans" title="History of New Orleans">the history of New Orleans (q.v.)</a>, among them those by Henry Rightor and John Smith Kendall provide background.</li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louisiana&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
